  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_594e8bbb-9a2b-41df-a4f9-f3611450b720'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8733033777446464998">In simple, mesmerizing prose, Hermann Hesse's <i>Journey to the East</i> tells of a journey both geographic and spiritual. H.H., a German choirmaster, is invited on an expedition with the League, a secret society whose members include Paul Klee, Mozart, and Albertus Magnus. The participants traverse both space and time, encountering Noah's Ark in Zurich and Don Quixote at Bremgar</span>
  <span id="freeText8733033777446464998" style="display:none">In simple, mesmerizing prose, Hermann Hesse's <i>Journey to the East</i> tells of a journey both geographic and spiritual. H.H., a German choirmaster, is invited on an expedition with the League, a secret society whose members include Paul Klee, Mozart, and Albertus Magnus. The participants traverse both space and time, encountering Noah's Ark in Zurich and Don Quixote at Bremgarten. The pilgrims' ultimate destination is the East, the "Home of the Light," where they expect to find spiritual renewal. Yet the harmony that ruled at the outset of the trip soon degenerates into an opening conflict. Each traveler finds the rest of the group intolerable and heads off in his own direction, with H.H. bitterly blaming the others for the failure of the journey. It is only long after the trip, while poring over records in the League archives, that H.H. discovers his own role in the dissolution of the group, and the ominous significance of the journey itself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8733033777446464998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

what is &quot;the expedition&quot;?
why is it?
it exists, Yes, but is it necessary?
who should be the members? 
Can they succeed?
the ultimate path for the journey has been proven to be out of human sight
solution: human turns into some thing els
but the novel is written within the current domain, 
so the novel is a useless trial , 
however it is consequently the writer&#39;s best reach</a>

<div id="review_443918334" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/443918334" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/443918334?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6683877">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer443918334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13274907221884227891">Die Morgenlandfahrt = The Journey to the East, Hermann Hesse <br/>Journey to the East is a short novel by German author Hermann Hesse. It was first published in German in 1932 as "Die Morgenlandfahrt". This novel came directly after his biggest international success, Narcissus and Goldmund. Journey to the East is written from the point of view of a man (in the book called "H. H.") who becomes a member of "The League", a timeless religious sect whose members include famous fictional and real character</span>
  <span id="freeText13274907221884227891" style="display:none">Die Morgenlandfahrt = The Journey to the East, Hermann Hesse <br />Journey to the East is a short novel by German author Hermann Hesse. It was first published in German in 1932 as "Die Morgenlandfahrt". This novel came directly after his biggest international success, Narcissus and Goldmund. Journey to the East is written from the point of view of a man (in the book called "H. H.") who becomes a member of "The League", a timeless religious sect whose members include famous fictional and real characters, such as Plato, Mozart, Pythagoras, Paul Klee, Don Quixote, Puss in Boots, Tristram Shandy, Baudelaire, Goldmund (from Hesse's Narcissus and Goldmund), the artist Klingsor (from Hesse's Klingsor's Last Summer), and the ferryman Vasudeva (from Hesse's Siddhartha). A branch of the group goes on a pilgrimage to "the East" in search of the "ultimate Truth". The narrator speaks of traveling through both time and space, across geography imaginary and real.<br /><br />عنوانها: سفر به شرق؛ راهیان اقلیم رنگ و نور خاور؛ سفر به سوی شرق؛ سفر به سوی صبح؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان آلمانی سده 20 م؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بیست و چهارم ماه آوریل سال 1984 میلادی<br />عنوان: سفر به شرق؛ نویسنده: هرمان هسه؛ مترجم: سروش حبیبی؛ در 127 ص؛ چاپ دیگر با عنوان سفر به سوی صبح: تهران، انتشارات ماهی؛ 1390؛ شابک: 9789642090792؛ چاپ دوم 1392؛ شابک: 9789642091614؛<br />عنوان: سفر به شرق؛ نویسنده: هرمان هسه؛ مترجم: علی قائم مقامی؛ تهران، گام، 1362؛ در 120 ص<br />عنوان: سفر به شرق؛ نویسنده: هرمان هسه؛ مترجم: کیانوش هدایت؛ تهران، مجید، 1377، در 160 ص، چاپ دوم 1388؛ شابک: 9789644530159؛ عنوان دیگر: راهیان اقلیم رنگ و نور خاور؛<br />عنوان: سفر به سوی شرق؛ نویسنده: هرمان هسه؛ مترجم: عبدالحسین شریفیان؛ تهران، اساطیر، 1378، در 128 ص، چاپ دوم 1388؛ شابک: 9645960886؛ <br />عنوان: سفر به سوی شرق؛ نویسنده: هرمان هسه؛ مترجم: محمد بقایی (ماکان)؛ تهران، انتشارات تهران، 1388، در 108 ص، چاپ دوم 1393؛ شابک: 97896452911264؛<br /><br />سفر به شرق، عجیب‌ترین و خیال‌ انگیزترین رمان «هرمان هسه» است، که از عالم واقع به دنیای خیال می‌رود، و ظرایف روح انسان را بیان می‌دارد. در قالب رومانتیسم عرضه شده، بنیان اصلی این داستان بر کشف ظرایف روح انسان قرار دارد، ولی نمی‌توان آن را یک اثر فلسفی دانست. جستجوی عارفانه‌ ای که در کالبد این اثر وجود دارد، پای استدلال و عقل به دلیل نااستواری به حقیقت راه ندارد. این داستان سرانجام با یادآوری این نکته به پایان می‌رسد که در طریق حقیقت، بی‌دلیل راه، نمی‌توان به مقصود رسید، و هر اهتمامی، بی‌ حاصل می‌شود. این نکته که شواهد آن را، در عرفان ایرانی نیز، می‌توان بسیار یافت، یادآور قواعد پذیرفته شده ی مرید و مراد، در دنیای عرفان است. ا. شربیانی</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_565935157" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/565935157" />
    <a title="Ben Winch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5647943-ben-winch"><img alt="Ben Winch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605154548p2/5647943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/565935157?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben Winch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben Winch" href="/user/show/5647943-ben-winch">Ben Winch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=mainland-european">mainland-european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647943-ben-winch?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer565935157" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6268587862052663958">This is an anomaly in Hesse’s oeuvre – a personal piece in which he risks alienating his wider audience, and yet in another sense his most universal work. It’s true, I say this having had few successes in recommending it, yet so far no-one I’ve given it to has <i>disliked</i> it, even if it has left them frustrated or puzzled or underwhelmed. The crux of it is, it’s the story of a <i>failure</i>. An inevitable failure, I would say, but as Hesse himself says early in the piece, “the seemingly impossible must c</span>
  <span id="freeText6268587862052663958" style="display:none">This is an anomaly in Hesse’s oeuvre – a personal piece in which he risks alienating his wider audience, and yet in another sense his most universal work. It’s true, I say this having had few successes in recommending it, yet so far no-one I’ve given it to has <i>disliked</i> it, even if it has left them frustrated or puzzled or underwhelmed. The crux of it is, it’s the story of a <i>failure</i>. An inevitable failure, I would say, but as Hesse himself says early in the piece, “the seemingly impossible must continually be attempted”. What, then, is the seemingly impossible attempt made here? It’s twofold: the telling of an untellable story, the making of an impossible journey. That the narrator fails in the telling should not surprise us; he warns us of this inevitability from the story’s start. That he has failed in his journey – though he himself, at first, is unaware of it – is also unsurprising, given that the journey’s goal is spiritual enlightenment, the absolute, a realm denied to humans except in glimpses.<br /><br />So. I feel keenly the irony of my reviewing this book as I sit in this far-from-perfect setting and write this. Like H.H., the narrator of <i>The Journey...</i>, I am depressed, self-pitying, unable to grasp with the greatest effort what once came so naturally, and sitting in the courtyard of a small-town cafe while children scream, dogs bark and a table full of bovine suburban-cum-country folk unfurl punchline after punchline at the next table, laughing uproariously. Like H.H., I am also without music, having left my i-Pod at home through some oversight, and back home are three children not my own, two of whom, I’ll wager, are screaming, shouting and brawling as I write this, unless the television is on, in which case they’re silent but may well emerge more irritable than before, positive ions and escapism being, like all drugs, imperfect balm, after all... And then there’s this book – this brief book in which I’ve sought my own balm for twenty years or more, having read it five, maybe six times since I first found it in a secondhand store in Adelaide in my late teens. <br /><br />The book! It’s personal. Hesse had tried something like this before, with <i>Steppenwolf</i>, when he submitted to his publishers a collection of ultra-personal poems which he intended to accompany the novel, but these were deemed too indulgent, too angry, too obscure for a wider readership, and were held back to be published separately in a limited edition. So with <i>The Journey...</i>, I guess Hesse put his foot down, determined to speak from his heart with as little translation as possible. And the result, to the casual reader, can admittedly be baffling. But even to the teenage me, it wasn’t alienating. Just <i>read over</i> the references that make no sense. The important part – the universal part – is the story of faith gained, lost and gained again. And the failure is just a part of the cycle. The two characters – H.H. and Leo – are mirror images, two parts of a whole, at least symbolically, and Leo’s apparent desertion (later revealed to be anything but) is the point at which faith becomes despair. H.H., despairing, self-absorbed; Leo, faithful, selfless. H.H., author, mortal; Leo, character, immortal. Read this way, the ending is uplifting, not a fade-to-grey. And the story is a dream-picture of sleep and awakening.<br /><br />Ugh. I’m aware that as a review this makes about as much sense as <i>The Journey...</i> makes as a novel. Novel? I don’t even know if it <i>is</i> a novel. Novella, maybe. And a novella in which you won’t find a three-dimensional character or more than one or two niceties of plot: writer and ex-journeyer attempts and fails to write the story of a failed journey, but in the process reveals the truth about that failure. Like all of Hesse’s stories, it’s a story of self-discovery. Like <i>Steppenwolf</i> (whose narrator, Harry Haller, is another H.H.), it’s also a fairly naked and often despairing self-portrait. Yet it takes us one step beyond that despair and self-absorption – takes us to the brink of its demise, once and for all, in Hesse’s fiction. And in showing an awakening from the inside out it achieves something difficult and valuable and profound. <br /><br />And besides, it’s beautiful. Unique. Magical. All things my teenage self understood perfectly, even as he struggled with the rest of it. If what you value in fiction – and in Hesse – is instinctive striving after enlightenment, it’s for you. That hallucination at the end of <i>Siddhartha</i> – that’s what I love in Hesse, and it’s in its most potent form here. A classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6268587862052663958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1316787216" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1316787216" />
    <a title="Susan Budd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44131812-susan-budd"><img alt="Susan Budd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434841111p2/44131812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1316787216?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Budd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Budd" href="/user/show/44131812-susan-budd">Susan Budd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553073621" href="/book/show/24859.The_Journey_To_The_East">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44131812-susan-budd?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1316787216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2444479124755411295">When I first read <i>The Journey to the East</i> in my youth, I was not ready for it. Having just reread it, I must confess that I am still not ready for it. But I am, at least, a little closer to being ready. Hopefully one day I will be ready and then I will read it and smile a wise smile of understanding. I suspect there will also be peace and contentment in my smile and that I will know who I am and why I am here. But for now, I can only appreciate the wisdom that I half-understand.<br/><br/>Perhaps you, dear</span>
  <span id="freeText2444479124755411295" style="display:none">When I first read <i>The Journey to the East</i> in my youth, I was not ready for it. Having just reread it, I must confess that I am still not ready for it. But I am, at least, a little closer to being ready. Hopefully one day I will be ready and then I will read it and smile a wise smile of understanding. I suspect there will also be peace and contentment in my smile and that I will know who I am and why I am here. But for now, I can only appreciate the wisdom that I half-understand.<br /><br />Perhaps you, dear reader, think I really do understand. That is what I thought when I read it the first time, for I understood the words that were printed on the page. But that is only further proof that I was not ready for it. Now, with the knowledge that I am still not ready, I am closer than I was when I thought I understood. <br /><br />I feel a kinship with H.H. His weaknesses are my weaknesses. His desires my desires ~ especially his desire to record his journey. And his despair is my despair. Unlike <i>Demian</i> and <i>Siddhartha</i>, this is not a young person’s book. <i>The Journey to the East</i> is about the failure of H.H. ~ not only the failure he recognizes, but also the failure he does not recognize. And it is the failure he does not recognize which is the serious one. <br /><br />Rereading this book now, at the same age Hesse was when he published it, I must acknowledge my own desertion of the journey, my own forgetfulness and unfaithfulness to the league. I even sold my violin ~ figuratively speaking. And now I long to return. Perhaps my poetic creations, humble though they may be, will one day be more real than I am. Perhaps they already are, and when the last drop of that which is me flows into them, I will be able to lie down and sleep.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2444479124755411295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_596405219" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/596405219" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/596405219?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=general-novels">general-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer596405219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9096923119973303497">I have enjoyed the novels I have read by Hesse, but this wasn't really one that resonated with me. The narrator H.H joins a quasi-religious organisation called The League which has ancient roots and members from reality and fiction: Plato, Don Quixote, Mozart, Tristram Shandy, Baudelaire, Puss in Boots (I kid you not). There is a pilgrimage to the East, which falls apart when a servant called Leo seems to disappear. Of Course, Leo is much more than a servant as the rest of the novella reveals, w</span>
  <span id="freeText9096923119973303497" style="display:none">I have enjoyed the novels I have read by Hesse, but this wasn't really one that resonated with me. The narrator H.H joins a quasi-religious organisation called The League which has ancient roots and members from reality and fiction: Plato, Don Quixote, Mozart, Tristram Shandy, Baudelaire, Puss in Boots (I kid you not). There is a pilgrimage to the East, which falls apart when a servant called Leo seems to disappear. Of Course, Leo is much more than a servant as the rest of the novella reveals, with reflections on the master/servant role. After some years of despair and doubt about The League H.H finds his way back (via Leo, of course) and reaches a level of enlightenment and self awareness.<br />For me, it's all a little self absorbed. It isn't helped by Timothy Leary's rather lengthy and overblown introduction. H.H is, for me, too one-dimensional and taken up with concepts, inner journeys and completely unrelated to other members of the human race. The philosophy of life, personally, is too unrelated to interpersonal relationships and too obsessed with the inner journey and self actualisation. <br />However having had to read about Servant-Leadership management theories and having on a daily basis to manage a team of people, it was interesting to look at the character of Leo and his changing (yet being the same) role. <br />It's very brief and probably a good starting point for religious and philosophical arguments. I enjoyed Steppenwolf much more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9096923119973303497" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_151926498" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151926498" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151926498?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-3-stars">reviews-3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=cul-poli-phil-art">cul-poli-phil-art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2013">read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=hesse">hesse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151926498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4271005851717184765"><b>"Poet of the Interior Journey"</b><br/><br/>There was a time in my 20’s when I was obsessed with Hermann Hesse. I was a Hesse Obsessor. After all, he was regarded highly enough as an author to win the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1946.<br/><br/>Something now lures me back to the novels I read then, "Siddhartha" and "Steppenwolf". However, I thought I would try this one as a "wedgie" or stopgap between more ambitious projects.<br/><br/>In truth, this is more a novella than a novel.<br/><br/>Even burdened by a 30 page introduction by Dr Ti</span>
  <span id="freeText4271005851717184765" style="display:none"><b>"Poet of the Interior Journey"</b><br /><br />There was a time in my 20’s when I was obsessed with Hermann Hesse. I was a Hesse Obsessor. After all, he was regarded highly enough as an author to win the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1946.<br /><br />Something now lures me back to the novels I read then, "Siddhartha" and "Steppenwolf". However, I thought I would try this one as a "wedgie" or stopgap between more ambitious projects.<br /><br />In truth, this is more a novella than a novel.<br /><br />Even burdened by a 30 page introduction by Dr Timothy Leary (he coined the term "Poet of the Interior Journey" for Hesse), it’s less than 110 pages long.<br /><br />So, is it any good? Yes, well, it's OK.<br /><br /><b>The Home of Light</b><br /><br />There is a suggestion in the title of the novel that, in order to gain spiritual awareness, you must head towards the East.<br /><br />However, this is not a purely geographical concept. For the West, it doesn’t necessarily mean Asia. It is a metaphor: <br /><br /><i>"We not only wandered through Space, but also through Time. We moved towards the East, but we also travelled into the Middle Ages and the Golden Age."</i><br /><br />The East is where the Sun rises. The East is the Home of Light, the Home of Enlightenment. Even more simply, it is Home:<br /><br /><i>"Throughout the centuries it had been on the way, towards light and wonder, and each member, each group, indeed our whole host and its great pilgrimage, was only a wave in the eternal stream of human beings, of the eternal strivings of the human spirit towards the East, towards Home."</i><br /><br />Wisdom and spirituality are not just found in the East, they are found at Home.<br /><br /><b>Lost Pilgrims</b><br /><br />One other thing is implied: we can embark on our spiritual journey individually or we can travel as a collective.<br /><br />Whichever way we choose, each of us can stray and end up a lost pilgrim. <br /><br />The collective pilgrimage of Hesse's characters appears to fail and they feel disillusioned, worthless and spiritless:<br /><br /><i>"There was nothing else left for me to do but to satisfy my last desire: to let myself fall from the edge of the world into the void – to death."</i><br /><br />For them, the confrontation with the void ushers in a suicidal impulse.<br /><br /><b>The Inevitability of Despair</b><br /><br />All along, there is but one enemy, Despair.<br /><br />The protagonist HH’s ambition to write a book about his adventures is based on his desire to escape from Despair:<br /><br /><i>"It was the only means of saving me from nothingness, chaos and suicide."</i><br /><br />Despair is not just the experience of Depression for an Individual. It is not just something that the mentally imbalanced suffer from. <br /><br />All of us have to confront Despair every step of our spiritual journey. In Hesse's eyes, it's a necessary part of the journey:<br /><br /><i>"Despair is the result of each earnest attempt to go through life with virtue, justice and understanding and to fulfill their requirements. Children live on one side of despair, the awakened on the other side."</i><br /><br /><b>The Freedom to be Happy</b><br /><br />Along HH’s path, he imagines the source of his temporal Happiness:<br /><br /><i>"My happiness arose from the freedom to experience everything imaginable simultaneously to exchange outward and inward easily, to move Time and Space about like scenes in a theatre."</i><br /><br />Note the fluidity, not just of Space, but of Time, hence the earlier allusion to the Middle Ages and the Golden Age.<br /><br />You can see the appeal to Timothy Leary, who speculated [inaccurately in my opinion] that Hesse wrote the novella while on drugs.<br /><br /><b>Home is Where the Soul Is</b><br /><br />Once again, Hesse's spiritual journey transcends geography:<br /><br /><i>"Our goal was not only the East, or rather the East was not only a country and something geographical, but it was the home and youth of the soul, it was everywhere and nowhere, it was the union of all times."</i><br /><br />The Journey to the East is not just a journey to Asia, but an Interior Journey, a Journey that begins and ends at Home and with the Self.<br /><br />This is where we will find true Happiness.<br /><br /><b>The Disappearing Self</b><br /><br />In any spiritual journey, as with any other, we have to be cautious of spoilers.<br /><br />However, within the theistic framework of the novel, each individual member of the group must merge with the God figure:<br /><br /><i>"He must grow, I must disappear."</i><br /><br />The enemy of Spirituality is the persistence of the Self or Selfishness.<br /><br />Ultimately, it seems that Hesse’s message is that we must transcend the Self, embrace a Universal Love and become one with that Love, if you like, a God.<br /><br />We don't need to go elsewhere to achieve this.<br /><br />The best place to seek the Self and Universal Love is at Home, the Home of the Soul.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4271005851717184765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15185680815053755432" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1113469.Hermann_Hesse" title="Hermann Hesse" rel="nofollow noopener">Hermann Hesse</a> writes as though his words are god's perspective, but I don't believe in god... <div id="review_2230682697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2230682697" />
    <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi"><img alt="Glenn Sumi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444333567p2/28854579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2230682697?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Sumi" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi">Glenn Sumi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374500368" href="/book/show/1483370.The_Journey_to_the_East">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28854579-glenn-sumi?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28854579-glenn-sumi?shelf=1900-1960">1900-1960</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28854579-glenn-sumi?shelf=not-usa-can-uk">not-usa-can-uk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2230682697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6885137423941431701">This probably shouldn’t have been my introduction to Hermann Hesse’s work, but what can you do? I saw the modest little volume at the library and thought: “Gee, I should probably read <i>Steppenwolf</i> or <i>Demian</i> first, but why not whet my appetite with this? One book by the Nobel Prize winner should give me a taste of his genius, right?”<br/><br/>Um, not quite. It’s an opaque, confounding book about a man named H.H. (I’m assuming it’s partly autobiographical) who looks back on his time in a mysterious group cal</span>
  <span id="freeText6885137423941431701" style="display:none">This probably shouldn’t have been my introduction to Hermann Hesse’s work, but what can you do? I saw the modest little volume at the library and thought: “Gee, I should probably read <i>Steppenwolf</i> or <i>Demian</i> first, but why not whet my appetite with this? One book by the Nobel Prize winner should give me a taste of his genius, right?”<br /><br />Um, not quite. It’s an opaque, confounding book about a man named H.H. (I’m assuming it’s partly autobiographical) who looks back on his time in a mysterious group called “the League.” Their purpose was to journey to the East – which I assume is both a geographical and a spiritual destination involving enlightenment.<br /><br />Here’s a quote about the group’s journey:<br /><br /><i>… we not only wandered through Space, but also through Time. We moved towards the East, but we also travelled into the Middle ages and the Golden Age; we roamed through Italy or Switzerland, but at times we also spent the night in the tenth century and dwelt with the patriarchs or the fairies.</i><br /><br />Um, okay, H.H. If you say so.<br /><br />H.H. also tells us that members of this League included real people (Plato, Mozart, Paul Klee) and fictional ones (Don Quixote, Tristram Shandy, characters from Hesse’s books). Perhaps the book should have been called <i>The Journey To Self-Aggrandizement</i>.<br /><br />Anyhow, for some reason the group splintered after a simple, humble servant named Leo abruptly disappeared. Years later, H.H. meets a person who instantly makes him recall the League, and eventually, after some weird sort of trial, he learns a valuable lesson. Which presumably he’s passing on to us in this book.<br /><br />I suppose if you’re heavily into Eastern religion, this book will have meaning for you. Two of my grandparents were Buddhists, and I find it a very soothing, non-judgemental religion. Parts of this book reminded me of what Salinger’s Franny experienced as a pilgrim.<br /><br />Beyond that, I can’t say. But then again, I’ve never travelled to the Middle Ages, so what do I know? Nada, obviously. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6885137423941431701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_334736470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/334736470" />
    <a title="J.G. Keely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely"><img alt="J.G. Keely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/334736470?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.G. Keely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.G. Keely" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely">J.G. Keely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer334736470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2787485554686467679">Why is Hesse's concept of enlightenment indistinguishable from mental illness? First, in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53803341' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Glass Bead Game</a>, we get the depiction of a 'secular saint', and the signs of his enlightenment are that he has stopped all his creative work, often sits lost in thought, making no sign he understands anyone speaking to him, and when he does respond, it is with a brief non-sequitur. He otherwise wanders the gardens day and night with a bland smile frozen to his face. Perhaps it's only me who looks at thos</span>
  <span id="freeText2787485554686467679" style="display:none">Why is Hesse's concept of enlightenment indistinguishable from mental illness? First, in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53803341" rel="nofollow noopener">The Glass Bead Game</a>, we get the depiction of a 'secular saint', and the signs of his enlightenment are that he has stopped all his creative work, often sits lost in thought, making no sign he understands anyone speaking to him, and when he does respond, it is with a brief non-sequitur. He otherwise wanders the gardens day and night with a bland smile frozen to his face. Perhaps it's only me who looks at those symptoms and sees not enlightenment, but full-fledged dementia.<br /><br />In this work, we get a picture of a secret organization of enlightened individuals who seem to be a collection of homeless vagrants that wander the countryside obsessed with certain mythical objects, and convinced that an ancient, powerful conspiracy is running the world. Once again, my brain keeps telling me that Hesse must be writing satire, since there is nothing that separates this vision of enlightenment from mental disorder.<br /><br />The secret organization itself is the most interesting part of the narrative. It is a fantasy of magic, time travel, and Illuminist philosophy reminiscent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49482024" rel="nofollow noopener">Italo Calvino's 'magical realism'</a>. This odd vision of a world- and time-spanning sect of spiritual sorcerers was the most enjoyable and promising aspect of the book, so it was disappointing to me that it served only as a backdrop for a fairly bland story.<br /><br />The narrative is also full of allusions to various historical and literary figures, events, mythologies, and philosophies, but I didn't feel that Hesse did enough to connect them together into something meaningful. As usual, his spiritual philosophy was only as powerful as its vagueness. I did like the notion of a narrative which created allusive meaning like a metaphysical poem--combining references with a central argument to create depth--but Hesse failed to resolve it into anything so insightful.<br /><br />The weakest aspect of his presentation was the single-voiced, confessional style--something like a journal. Our narrator is constantly referencing interesting things that happened to him, but we don't actually get to experience them or understand them. Once again, vagueness is mistaken for profundity.<br /><br />I would have been interested in seeing more of this journey, and the odd experiences that made it up, instead of them being merely name-dropped. I'm not saying Hesse should have made everything clear or provided some grand meaning--I think an in-depth description of these fantastical events would have helped deepen his conceptual world, and provide for the reader symbolic examples to help lead us along.<br /><br />It's like those Lovecraft stories where the hero says 'the vision was too horrible to describe, its terror was beyond the meagre power of words to encapsulate it'--but then Lovecraft usually goes on to explain it, anyways--or at least he has an exciting, fast-paced story to make up for it. No such luck in Hesse.<br /><br />Once again we have a central, masterful figure who knows all but reveals little--the notion of the great teacher who has the greatest of reputations, despite the fact that we never see him do anything to deserve it. Hesse helpfully tells us that people like him and feel comfortable around him, but I wish he had just made the reader feel that way about him instead of trying to convince us of the inner life of a flat character. If you cannot believably write the Master, then do not make him a character. As depicted, he could have easily been a charlatan as a guru.<br /><br />Once again, I am reminded why I do not find bland spiritual wonderment enticing: the world is full of joy and wonder and mystery in infinite variations, so it always feels petty and false to me to try to encapsulate that in a vague symbolic experience, asking no questions and revealing nothing. I find it more enlightening to read an author with a hundred powerful and contradictory insights rather than a single, unified, featureless vision like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2787485554686467679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="L.S. Popovich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="L.S. Popovich" href="/user/show/4567704-l-s-popovich">L.S. Popovich</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4567704-l-s-popovich?shelf=2020">2020</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2910713638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11526604256043141433">The astounding vagueness of this short novel probably conceals a spiritual allegory under the guise of a universal message, though it is still too cryptic. It takes on new meaning when you realize Hesse was engaged in antiwar activities and was driven out of Germany during WWI. He became a naturalized citizen of Switzerland and eventually won the Nobel Prize. Perhaps some of his writings might have benefitted from a freer hand.<br/><br/>Combining many disparate literary and historical references, dashes o</span>
  <span id="freeText11526604256043141433" style="display:none">The astounding vagueness of this short novel probably conceals a spiritual allegory under the guise of a universal message, though it is still too cryptic. It takes on new meaning when you realize Hesse was engaged in antiwar activities and was driven out of Germany during WWI. He became a naturalized citizen of Switzerland and eventually won the Nobel Prize. Perhaps some of his writings might have benefitted from a freer hand.<br /><br />Combining many disparate literary and historical references, dashes of wisdom, and a blurring of the line between history and fable, this is a chronicle of inspiration, like many of Hesse's other books. He's enchanted by the East, and nostalgic for the Golden Age, but the geography of the region of his obsession is largely a mystery. Here is the trademark mysticism and the prospect of a loss of faith and the awakening of a spirit: <br /><br />"Despair is the result of each earnest attempt to go through a life with virtue, justice and understanding... Children live on one side of despair, and the awakened on the other."<br /><br />His lack of definitive time and place, and the lack of defining characteristics of the "League" that provides the impetus for this journey limit the reader's experience of the ideas Hesse wishes to explore. This is easily overshadowed by his more complex books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11526604256043141433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3439182431295608469" style="display:none">Journey to the East is written from the point of view of a man called H. H. He joins an organization called “The League", a religious sect made up of whose famous fictional and real characters. The journey starts out well with the group f members enthusiastic and happy. However, a crisis occurs at a mountain gorge called Morbio Inferiore. Leo apparently just a servant disappears which results in the group falling into crisis and ultimately breaking apart causing the group to break up losing their belief in the league. <br /><br />H.H wanders around for years in depression and a sense of failure until he meets Leo again. Then H.H must face a trial and test of his faith. The story is a Christian allegory where H.H faith is tested. Its a story about failure, depression, hope and ultimately in what you believe and how do you want to live. </span>

<div id="review_558794028" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558794028" />
    <a title="Lynne King" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15736557-lynne-king"><img alt="Lynne King" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357126217p2/15736557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558794028?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynne King" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynne King" href="/user/show/15736557-lynne-king">Lynne King</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15736557-lynne-king?shelf=definitely-to-read">definitely-to-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15736557-lynne-king?shelf=h-hesse">h-hesse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558794028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16566527624892327729">How does one begin a review of Hermann Hesse’s work? My first experience of his books was with “The Glass Bead Game”, the content of which fascinated me at the time and I found it easy to read. However, this book has really got me thinking and much as I like it, I wonder if the author is playing with the reader?<br/><br/>This is a spiritual journey of a German choirmaster called H.H. (could this be the author himself?) who unsuccessfully attempts to write about the “great journey” he made when he joined t</span>
  <span id="freeText16566527624892327729" style="display:none">How does one begin a review of Hermann Hesse’s work? My first experience of his books was with “The Glass Bead Game”, the content of which fascinated me at the time and I found it easy to read. However, this book has really got me thinking and much as I like it, I wonder if the author is playing with the reader?<br /><br />This is a spiritual journey of a German choirmaster called H.H. (could this be the author himself?) who unsuccessfully attempts to write about the “great journey” he made when he joined the “League, a religious organization. However, he’s handicapped because of his former vow to the League to never disclose anything about it at all.<br /><br />After a year’s probation he was asked what he hoped to gain from this journey towards the East into a legendary realm. His heart’s desire was to see the Princess Fatima. No problem and so he officially became a member of the League and was given a ring confirming his loyalty.<br /><br />I found the plot to be somewhat convoluted as H.H. sets off with a group heading towards “the East”. There are many groups and some get lost, never to surface again. There are incidents where those lost members have been trying to get back into the League but are unsuccessful. They can all travel through time as well as space, backwards and forwards and encounter real and imaginary individuals on the way, such as: Almansor and Parsifal, Witiko or Goldmund, Sancho Oanza, Don Quixote, Hoffman and the ferryman Vasudeva. Nevertheless, the writing style and descriptions are excellent.<br /><br />The catalyst to me in the book appears to be Leo, the servant to the group and yet “Leo knew all kinds of things; that he perhaps knew more than us, who were ostensibly his masters.”<br /><br />The group then falls into disarray when, upon arrival at the Morbio Inferiore Gorge on the Italian border, Leo disappears.<br /><br />Time relentlessly passes and H.H. decides that he must write about his own particular journey but he’s finding it more and more difficult to remember events. In particular he wondered about Leo. Through a friend he finds out, “We already have a Leo. Andreas Leo, 69a, Seilergraben.”<br /><br />The upshot is that this is the “proper” Leo but he apparently doesn’t remember H.H. who becomes so frustrated by this that he writes Leo a long letter. The consequence of this is that he’s taken to the League and finds out that Leo is in fact the president.<br /><br />This is also a book about lost opportunities as H.H. soon finds and as for the denouement, well, that was rather unexpected.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16566527624892327729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1650967973" />
    <a title="Benjamin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15767707-benjamin"><img alt="Benjamin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598186328p2/15767707.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1650967973?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin" href="/user/show/15767707-benjamin">Benjamin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15767707-benjamin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1650967973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16604424705282123552">Such a strange journey, such an elusive destination. This is equally horrible and mystical, and it is a somewhat difficult task to try and make sense of the ending. I found myself drawn to Leo immediately, while H.H. I could more easily identify with. This made it both confusing and slightly depressing. <br/><br/>It seems that life is much more magical and mystical in youth, and while I am still very young myself, I find that any excitement towards spirituality I once had, has been slowly replaced over th</span>
  <span id="freeText16604424705282123552" style="display:none">Such a strange journey, such an elusive destination. This is equally horrible and mystical, and it is a somewhat difficult task to try and make sense of the ending. I found myself drawn to Leo immediately, while H.H. I could more easily identify with. This made it both confusing and slightly depressing. <br /><br />It seems that life is much more magical and mystical in youth, and while I am still very young myself, I find that any excitement towards spirituality I once had, has been slowly replaced over the years with a jaded cynicism. At times I attribute this to growing up, and accepting only what is logical. But often I have strange epiphanies that paint truth as paradoxical. In these moments I think that not much can ever be truthfully revealed about the mysteries in life, although many people claim to have answers for these mysteries. And maybe the most frightening thing I think of in these moments, is that we might be grasping eternally at straws. Or looking for answers to questions that need none. <br /><br />Maybe the world is dictated by cold logic<br />Maybe it is governed by heartless chaos.<br />Maybe ordo ab chao <br />Maybe none of these things.<br />I'm in no position to say.<br /><br />Anyways I guess this isn't much of a review. This book just happened to get me thinking. <br />I hope it does the same for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16604424705282123552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2347091584" />
    <a title="Josh Caporale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5687551-josh-caporale"><img alt="Josh Caporale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1431172307p2/5687551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2347091584?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh Caporale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh Caporale" href="/user/show/5687551-josh-caporale">Josh Caporale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2347091584" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4442287329094659528">The Hermann Hesse novels that I have read thus far examined his philosophical, eastern inspired train of thought that took place upon his ability to seek enlightenment like that chronicled in Demian. Aside from Journey to the East and Demian, I have read Siddhartha and Narcissus and Goldmund. Demian remains my absolute favorite, but Journey to the East presented a great, cool breath of fresh air as well when it came to developing a greater understanding of one's quest for what they want in life </span>
  <span id="freeText4442287329094659528" style="display:none">The Hermann Hesse novels that I have read thus far examined his philosophical, eastern inspired train of thought that took place upon his ability to seek enlightenment like that chronicled in Demian. Aside from Journey to the East and Demian, I have read Siddhartha and Narcissus and Goldmund. Demian remains my absolute favorite, but Journey to the East presented a great, cool breath of fresh air as well when it came to developing a greater understanding of one's quest for what they want in life and the group of people with similar interests or motives they are willing to come together with in order to follow that pursuit in what is often recognized as religion. To me, my feelings for The Journey to the East were similar to that of Siddhartha, only The Journey to the East concentrated much more on delivering a message that was meant to directly hit the reader.<br /><br />H.H. is the central character in this piece, which we greatly assume is based on the fact that these are Hesse's very own initials. Hesse has placed himself in the central character's shoes before, notably in Demian as he took on the role of the author, Emil Sinclair. In this piece, though, H.H. joins the League in his pursuit for the beautiful Princess Fatima. I would argue that while religious pursuit and seeking either a higher being or enlightenment were the greater goals in this group, one is likely to join a group when looking for love. H.H. engages in several expeditions as the group makes their way to the east as if it is "home." Along the way, though, plenty of members give up on their pursuit, reflecting on the possibility that one may lose hope in a cause of this nature. Along the way, H.H. meets a servant named Leo, who possesses much more than what meets the eye.<br /><br />I always feel that I get something out of reading Hermann Hesse's books and this one is no different. I felt I got greater messages about our roles as people in society and how the world goes beyond who we are and what we want and how eventually we are tapped to play a pivotal role in the needs and desires of others, many of which are bigger and better than we will ever be. Some of this contributes to the greater good, while others just contribute, which is a key explanation in how our world works. This book was only 118 pages in league and brief enough in its subject matter, but like with most of Hesse's works, the philosophy and messages outweigh the storyline and plot. If done well, this is perfectly excusable. <br /><br />After reading this, I would definitely be keen to pick up more of what Hesse has to offer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4442287329094659528" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1516591097" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1516591097" />
    <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan"><img alt="Dhanaraj Rajan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433251321p2/4373783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1516591097?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhanaraj Rajan" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan">Dhanaraj Rajan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553073621" href="/book/show/24859.The_Journey_To_The_East">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=german-lit">german-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=nobel-prize">nobel-prize</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1516591097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer859974516414879855">I had read another book by Hesse prior to this - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1943736.Knulp__Picador_Books_' title='Knulp (Picador Books) by Hermann Hesse' rel='nofollow noopener'>Knulp</a>. The present book in review also shares the same quality that was found in the earlier book. That is, the book is relatively 'dull'. I mean the plot is not that riveting to keep the reader on the edge. Rather, the plot moves in its own pace and Hesse wants the reader to read between the lines.<br/><br/>As a result, a reader can come away from it carrying many messages. I came away with one and by sharing it I will end my review.<br/><br/>Hesse must have been ha</span>
  <span id="freeText859974516414879855" style="display:none">I had read another book by Hesse prior to this - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1943736.Knulp__Picador_Books_" title="Knulp (Picador Books) by Hermann Hesse" rel="nofollow noopener">Knulp</a>. The present book in review also shares the same quality that was found in the earlier book. That is, the book is relatively 'dull'. I mean the plot is not that riveting to keep the reader on the edge. Rather, the plot moves in its own pace and Hesse wants the reader to read between the lines.<br /><br />As a result, a reader can come away from it carrying many messages. I came away with one and by sharing it I will end my review.<br /><br />Hesse must have been haunted with a question: What is the meaning of life? In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1943736.Knulp__Picador_Books_" title="Knulp (Picador Books) by Hermann Hesse" rel="nofollow noopener">Knulp</a> Hesse merely said that one must find meaning of one's life before it ended and that would be the salvation. Here <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24859.The_Journey_To_The_East" title="The Journey To The East by Hermann Hesse" rel="nofollow noopener">The Journey To The East</a> Hesse attempts still further to find out the meaning of human life in an objective manner. He calls to his aid the spiritual traditions of both East and West. And the answer boils down to the first book of Genesis (at least to my point of view). Genesis 1: 27 reads thus: "And God created man in his own image, in the image of God created he him; male and female created he them."<br /><br />Man is called to live up to that calling. That is, he must realize that he is created in the image of God and live accordingly. As God loves everyone, he must love God and everyone. For everyone is an image of God. This easy calling may be hindered by our own image (ego). The ego searches for its own satisfaction which is not permanent. To be willing to cross over our image (ego) and to be willing to be the image of God is the source of our true happiness. God must increase and we must decrease.<br /><br />If one interprets it from the Indian point of view it would be like saying the following: the <i>Atman</i> should realize that it is the <i>Brahman</i>. Hesse might have been influenced by Vedanta philosophy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="859974516414879855" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36235849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1257683453547855215">Although the most difficult to comprehend of Hesse's novels, The Journey to the East was, upon reading, one of my favorites as it left me with some vague, yet powerful, apprehension of a vital truth--something like those essential words we find "at the tip of the tongue" but are unable to speak, those aspects of memory we know to be there but cannot recollect, those revelations conveyed in dreams, in deliria or under the influence of psychotropics.<br/><br/>Now, having substantially wasted my life and bei</span>
  <span id="freeText1257683453547855215" style="display:none">Although the most difficult to comprehend of Hesse's novels, The Journey to the East was, upon reading, one of my favorites as it left me with some vague, yet powerful, apprehension of a vital truth--something like those essential words we find "at the tip of the tongue" but are unable to speak, those aspects of memory we know to be there but cannot recollect, those revelations conveyed in dreams, in deliria or under the influence of psychotropics.<br /><br />Now, having substantially wasted my life and being of the same age as its author, I see The Journey as a reflection on my youth, particularly to the awakening of intellect and ethical aspirations upon becoming acquainted with the great, timeless world of ideas afforded by literature.<br /><br />This theme runs, of course, throughout much of Hesse's writing, culminating in The Glass Bead Game.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1269819562" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Po Po" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11138071-po-po"><img alt="Po Po" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426824435p2/11138071.jpg" /></a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1269819562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7765939547585001618"><br/>An incredible writer makes a truly depressing not-much-actually-happens story-story into something inspirational.<br/><br/>If you've ever found yourself wallowing in despair, utterly disconsolate, and demoralized, then this book is for you. <br/><br/>League brother H looks back upon his life and questions the meaning of his every choice and action. He finds himself in the throes of an existential crisis.<br/><br/>Insightful little book. Warning: Inconclusive ending.<br/><br/>Several main points:<br/><br/>+ Despair is the constant state of human</span>
  <span id="freeText7765939547585001618" style="display:none"><br />An incredible writer makes a truly depressing not-much-actually-happens story-story into something inspirational.<br /><br />If you've ever found yourself wallowing in despair, utterly disconsolate, and demoralized, then this book is for you. <br /><br />League brother H looks back upon his life and questions the meaning of his every choice and action. He finds himself in the throes of an existential crisis.<br /><br />Insightful little book. Warning: Inconclusive ending.<br /><br />Several main points:<br /><br />+ Despair is the constant state of humanity.<br /><br />+ The servant is master.<br /><br />+ Traveling to far-off places and reaching a 'destination' doesn't change your life or bring meaning, unless you are also willing to change how you perceive the world.<br /><br />+ The true journey takes place inside your head.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />+ "What person really knows another or even himself? As for me, I am not one who understands people at all."<br /><br />+ "So many come and go; one knows people and yet does not know them."<br /><br />+ "Everything is only a mass of fragmentary pictures which has been reflected in something, and this something is myself, and this self, this mirror, whenever I have gazed into it, has proved to be nothing but the uppermost surface of a glass pane."<br /><br />+ "Is your story capable of being told?"<br /><br />+ "Whatever happens, I have decided to exercise my will. Even if I have to re-commence my difficult story ten times, a hundred times, and always arrive at the same cul-de-sac, just the same I will begin again a hundred times."<br /><br />+ "I either had to write the book or be reduced to despair; it was the only means of saving me from nothingness, chaos and suicide."<br /><br />+ "Despair is the result of each earnest attempt to understand and vindicate human life."<br /><br />+ "Despair is the result of each earnest attempt to go through life with virtue, justice, and understanding and to fulfill their requirements."<br /><br />+ "Everything becomes questionable as soon as I consider it closely, everything slips away and dissolves.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7765939547585001618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17296521534803237611">I can't understand the appeal of Herman Hesse. I might be wrong, since he has won Nobel Prize and one person whose opinion I respect a lot has given this book five stars. However, no matter how hard I try not only I find Hesse a bad writer, I cringe at the idea of calling him a writer at all. To me he's no different from Paulo Coehlo. This book has all the things I hate in a "novel": disgusting, shallow, racist, and colonialist "eastern" "mysticism", no real plot, no real events, no real charact</span>
  <span id="freeText17296521534803237611" style="display:none">I can't understand the appeal of Herman Hesse. I might be wrong, since he has won Nobel Prize and one person whose opinion I respect a lot has given this book five stars. However, no matter how hard I try not only I find Hesse a bad writer, I cringe at the idea of calling him a writer at all. To me he's no different from Paulo Coehlo. This book has all the things I hate in a "novel": disgusting, shallow, racist, and colonialist "eastern" "mysticism", no real plot, no real events, no real characters, obvious and superficial symbolism, and a string of stomach-churning "inspirational" quotes that belong on the Facebook feed of people with no understanding of philosophy and religion, scrawled across a photo of a sunset or a rainforest, in sans-script font, not inside a novel.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_795534238" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/795534238" />
    <a title="S.Ach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/233507-s-ach"><img alt="S.Ach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515914630p2/233507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/795534238?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S.Ach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S.Ach" href="/user/show/233507-s-ach">S.Ach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/233507-s-ach?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/233507-s-ach?shelf=nobel-laureate">nobel-laureate</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/233507-s-ach?shelf=germany-austria">germany-austria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer795534238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14021677781652311820">I have read many books by many great minds. Sartre, Camus, Dostoevsky, Nobakov, Ayn Rand et al. I find an inner struggle to find out the various aspects of truth in their writing. Their works disturb us. They inspire us. They confuse us about ourselves. They make us question. And they propel us towards a self-exploring journey.<br/>But, reading Hesse is different. When I read him, I don't feel as if I am reading a literary expert or philosopher. I feel as I am with a saint. The man who has experience</span>
  <span id="freeText14021677781652311820" style="display:none">I have read many books by many great minds. Sartre, Camus, Dostoevsky, Nobakov, Ayn Rand et al. I find an inner struggle to find out the various aspects of truth in their writing. Their works disturb us. They inspire us. They confuse us about ourselves. They make us question. And they propel us towards a self-exploring journey.<br />But, reading Hesse is different. When I read him, I don't feel as if I am reading a literary expert or philosopher. I feel as I am with a saint. The man who has experienced the truth himself. For me, he brings in the difference between intellectualism and sainthood.<br />My first book of Hesse happens to be "Siddharth" which according to me a must read for all and sundry. With "Demian"(his debut novel), I found his self-discovery intent. "Narcissus and Goldmund" provides the perfect example of his profundity on the subject of mind and heart.<br /><br />"Journey to the East" displays his take on the oriental philosophy. The book is more of metaphysical symbolism than a spiritual adventure. The plot involves, a group of high intellectuals taking a journey both in space and time; that's why you will find Plato, Mozart and Don Quixote part of the same league as the protagonist, goes by the name HH (Hermann Hesse?) in their journey towards the East or finding the ultimate truth. Slowly the team disintegrates because of individualities. Towards the end when the self-deprecating HH, finds the truth, he is more than just amazed....<br /><br />This book shouldn't be taken as an introduction to Hesse ( though the small size might be tempting to take the risk).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14021677781652311820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
